<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41587"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Draft genome sequence of pigeonpea ( Cajanus cajan ), an orphan legume crop of resource-poor farmers"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Biotechnology 2011 30:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2011-11-06"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2011 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Pigeonpea is an important protein source in many developing countries, but limited genetic resources have constrained its improvement. The draft genome sequence of pigeonpea, the first for a nonindustrial crop and for a grain legume, should facilitate molecular breeding efforts to improve yields for subsistence farmers. Pigeonpea is an important legume food crop grown primarily by smallholder farmers in many semi-arid tropical regions of the world. We used the Illumina next-generation sequencing platform to generate 237.2 Gb of sequence, which along with Sanger-based bacterial artificial chromosome end sequences and a genetic map, we assembled into scaffolds representing 72.7% (605.78 Mb) of the 833.07 Mb pigeonpea genome. Genome analysis predicted 48,680 genes for pigeonpea and also showed the potential role that certain gene families, for example, drought tolerance&#8211;related genes, have played throughout the domestication of pigeonpea and the evolution of its ancestors. Although we found a few segmental duplication events, we did not observe the recent genome-wide duplication events observed in soybean. This reference genome sequence will facilitate the identification of the genetic basis of agronomically important traits, and accelerate the development of improved pigeonpea varieties that could improve food security in many developing countries."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>Pigeonpea is an important legume food crop grown primarily by smallholder farmers in many semi-arid tropical regions of the world. We used the Illumina next-generation sequencing platform to generate 237.2 Gb of sequence, which along with Sanger-based bacterial artificial chromosome end sequences and a genetic map, we assembled into scaffolds representing 72.7% (605.78 Mb) of the 833.07 Mb pigeonpea genome. Genome analysis predicted 48,680 genes for pigeonpea and also showed the potential role that certain gene families, for example, drought toleranceârelated genes, have played throughout the domestication of pigeonpea and the evolution of its ancestors. Although we found a few segmental duplication events, we did not observe the recent genome-wide duplication events observed in soybean. This reference genome sequence will facilitate the identification of the genetic basis of agronomically important traits, and accelerate the development of improved pigeonpea varieties that could improve food security in many developing countries.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    </div>
                
                
                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Pigeonpea (<i>Cajanus cajan</i> L.), a diploid legume crop species (2<i>n</i> = 2<i>x</i> = 22), is a member of the tribe <i>Phaseoleae.</i> This tribe is located in the millettioid (tropical) clade within the subfamily <i>Papilionoideae</i>, which includes many important legume crop species such as soybean (<i>Glycine max</i>), cowpea (<i>Vigna ungiculata</i>), common bean (<i>Phaseolus vulgaris</i>) and mung bean (<i>Vigna radiata</i>). The sister galegoid (temperate) clade also contains many important legume crops such as alfalfa (<i>Medicago sativa</i>), chickpea (<i>Cicer arietinum</i>), clover (<i>Trifolium</i> spp.), pea (<i>Pisum sativum</i>), lentil (<i>Lens culinaris</i>) as well as barrel medic (<i>Medicago truncatula</i>) and lotus (<i>Lotus japonicus</i>). The last two have emerged as important model species for understanding legume genomics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Cannon, S.B., May, G.D. &amp; Jackson, S.A. Three sequenced legume genomes and many crop species: rich opportunities for translational genomics. Plant Physiol. 151, 970â977 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d6311e925">1</a></sup>.</p><p>Pigeonpea is grown on <span class="stix">â¼</span>5 million hectares (ha), making it the sixth most important legume food crop globally. Domesticated &gt;3,500 years ago in India<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Vavilov, N.I. The origin, variation, immunity, and breeding of cultivated plants. Chron. Bot. 13, 1â366 (1951)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d6311e932">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="De, D.N. Pigeonpea. in Evolutionary Studies in World Crops: Diversity and Change in the Indian Subcontinent (ed., Hutchinson, J.). 79â87 (Cambridge University Press, London, 1974)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d6311e935">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Royes, W.V. Pigeonpea. in Evolution of Crop Plants (ed., Sommonds, N.W.). 154â156 (Longmans, London and New York, 1976)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d6311e938">4</a></sup>, it is the main protein source for more than a billion people in the developing world and a cash crop that supports the livelihoods of millions of resource-poor farmers in Asia, Africa, South America, Central America and the Caribbean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mula, M.G. &amp; Saxena, K.B. Lifting the Level of Awareness on Pigeonpeaâa Global Perspective (International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics, 2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6311e942">5</a></sup>. In the developing world, protein is often only available at levels less than one-third of minimum dietary requirements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Latham, M.C. Human nutrition in the developing world. FAO Food and Nutrition Series No. 29 (UN Food and Agriculture Organization, 1997) ã
                    http://www.fao.org/DOCREP/W0073e/w0073e05.htm
                    
                  ã." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d6311e946">6</a></sup>, and without improvements in agricultural productivity, this challenge is likely to worsen due to increases in human population and crop yield stagnation. From a food security perspective, legumes provide a highly balanced and nutritious source of calories and protein that is not provided by cereals, especially those commonly grown in semi-arid regions.</p><p>Owing to biotic and abiotic stresses, and the fact that pigeonpea is grown in low-input and risk-prone marginal environments, there is a large gap between potential yield (2,500 Kg/ha) and yields obtained on farmer's fields (866.2 kg/ha in Asia and 736.2 kg/ha in Africa)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Mula, M.G. &amp; Saxena, K.B. Lifting the Level of Awareness on Pigeonpeaâa Global Perspective (International Crops Research Institute for the Semi-Arid Tropics, 2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d6311e953">5</a></sup>. Together, limited genomic resources and low levels of genetic diversity in the primary gene pool have constrained genetic improvement of pigeonpea<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bohra, A. et al. Harnessing the potential of crop wild relatives through genomics tools for pigeonpea improvement. J. Plant Biol. 37, 85â100 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6311e957">7</a></sup>. It is one of a range of orphan (or neglected) crops that have not benefited from intensive scientific research despite their importance for regional food security in the world's poorest regions.</p><p>To accelerate the application of genomics to improve yield and quality, we generated and analyzed a draft genome sequence for the pigeonpea genotype ICPL 87119, popularly known as Asha (meaning hope in Hindi). This is an inbred line and a widely cultivated medium duration Indian variety resistant to several important diseases (<i>Fusarium</i> wilt (FW) and sterility mosaic disease (SMD)), for which a number of genetic and genomic resources have been recently developed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Varshney, R.K. et al. Pigeonpea genomics initiative (PGI): an international effort to improve crop productivity of pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan L.). Mol. Breed. 26, 393â408 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d6311e967">8</a></sup>. This is the first draft genome sequence for a grain legume as well as the first for an orphan legume crop and probably the first for a nonindustrial crop. It will help to increase the efficiency of pigeonpea improvement by integrating biotechnological tools in conventional breeding and the use of genome information of pigeonpea in other legume species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>We used the Illumina GA and HiSeq 2000 Sequencing system to sequence 11 small-insert (180â800 bp) and 11 large-insert (2â20 kb) libraries. This generated a total of 237.2 Gb of paired-end reads, ranging from 50â100 bp (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>). Filtering and correction of the sequence data for very small and/or bad-quality sequences yielded 130.7 Gb of high-quality sequence, <span class="stix">â¼</span>163.4Ã coverage of the pigeonpea genome. Analysis of sequence data for GC content indicated a similar GC content distribution in the genomes of pigeonpea and soybean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). Additionally, a set of 88,860 bacterial artificial chromosome (BAC) end sequences were generated using Sanger sequencing from two BAC libraries (69,120 clones) by using the HindIII (34,560 clones) and BamHI (34,560 clones) restriction enzymes.</p><p>We used the assembler SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6311e992">9</a></sup> to assemble 605.78 Mb of the pigeonpea genome <i>de novo</i>, generating a sequence with a contig N50 of 21.95 kb, and longest contig length of 185.39 kb. We then improved the assembly by using both the paired-BAC end sequences (41,302) that passed after filtering through RepeatMasker, and a genetic map comprising 833 marker loci (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). This increased N50 to 516.06 kb (longest scaffold in chromosome level of 48.97 Mb) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Our draft genome assembly has &lt;5.69% (<span class="stix">â¼</span>34 Mb) unclosed gaps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311â317 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1005">10</a></sup>. These analyses showed that mapped genetic loci provide additional information for assembling superscaffolds, especially in regions in which scaffolds were not large enough to cross the repeat rich regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). The generated chromosome-scale scaffolds can be considered as 'pseudomolecules' (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>). We estimated the pigeonpea genome size, based on K-mer statistics, to be 833.07 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), suggesting that the assembly captures 72.7% of the genome in the genome scaffolds. If only the 6,534 scaffolds &gt;2 kb are considered, the assembly spans 578 Mb with an N50 of 0.58 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Tab1">Table 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Assembly and annotation statistics for the pigeonpea genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2022/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Analysis of our genome assembly against the database of bacterial genomes using Megablast showed no contamination of any bacterial contig in the genome assembly. This was expected, as the GC-depth graph and distribution analysis of the genome data sets used for assembly did not show any characteristic feature of microbial genomes. In terms of checking the assembly for organellar DNA contamination, analysis of the soybean chloroplast DNA against the genome assembly showed hits with only 36 of 6,534 scaffolds (&gt;2 kb). The longest one is about 50 kb in the scaffold 000124. This observation is also not unexpected, as long stretches of chloroplast and mitochondrial DNA have been shown to be transferred into nuclear chromosomes of several plant and animal species during evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Timmis, J.N. et al. Endosymbiotic gene transfer: organelle genomes forge eukaryotic chromosomes. Nat. Rev. Genet. 5, 123â135 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1569">11</a></sup>.</p><p>The transcriptome assembly (CcTAv2.0, <a href="http://cajca.comparative-legumes.org/data/lista_cajca-201012.tgz">http://cajca.comparative-legumes.org/data/lista_cajca-201012.tgz</a>) composed of 21,434 contigs, referred to as transcriptome assembly contigs (TACs), was mapped to the draft genome assembly. Of the 21,434 TACs, 97% of the total length of them could be mapped to the genome assembly with &gt;90% sequence identity. We found 94.78% of TACs in the genome assembly at &gt;90% identity and &gt;50% coverage of query length. Using more stringent criteria (&gt;90% identity and &gt;90% of the coverage), 88.53% of TACs were captured (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). These results indicate an extremely low proportion of misassemblies, at least in the gene-rich regions. The mapping of the CcTAv2.0 was only for quality control purposes. The transcriptome assembly does not necessarily represent all pigeonpea genes; it is restricted to the tissue types used and genes expressed at low levels are likely to be under-represented.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Repetitive sequences</h3><p><i>De novo</i> repeat identification using RepeatModeler and homology analysis against the RepBase library identified repetitive DNA (excluding low-complexity sequences) in 51.67% of the genome, most of which could not be associated with known transposable element (TE) families. The fraction of repetitive sequences in the genome is comparable to other genomes, like those of soybean (59%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1596">12</a></sup>, castor bean (50%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chan, A.P. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 951â956 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1600">13</a></sup> and grapevine (41%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1604">14</a></sup>, but less than that seen in the genomes of maize (85%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1608">15</a></sup> and sorghum (62%)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Paterson, A.H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551â556 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1612">16</a></sup>. Classification of the observed transposable elements into known classes revealed that the majority of repetitive sequences were retrotransposons (37.12%), whereas 8.77% of the transposable elements were DNA transposons (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Tab2">Table 2</a>). Like the soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1620">12</a></sup> and castor bean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Chan, A.P. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 951â956 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1624">13</a></sup> genomes, the most abundant repeats identified are long-terminal repeat elements, of which 22.81% are <i>Gypsy</i>-type elements and 12.04% are <i>Copia</i>-type elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Tab2">Table 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-2"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab2" data-test="table-caption">Table 2 Repetitive sequences in the pigeonpea genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/nbt.2022/tables/2"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Gene annotation</h3><p>We used a combination of <i>de novo</i> gene prediction programs and homology-based methods to predict gene models in the pigeonpea genome. These were combined using the GLEAN algorithm<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1938">17</a></sup>, resulting in the identification of 48,680 genes with an average transcript length of 2,348.70 bp, coding sequence size of 959.35 bp and 3.59 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). The majority of these predicted genes (99.6%) were supported either by <i>de novo</i> gene prediction, expressed sequence tags (EST)/unigenes or homology-based searching, or a combination of these approaches (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). To further validate the gene predictions, we used the predicted pigeonpea gene set to search KOGs, the core genes from the core eukaryotic gene mapping approach (CEGMA) pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1952">18</a></sup>. The presence of 453 out of 458 (98.9%) KOGs within the pigeonpea gene set (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>) confirms that annotation of the pigeonpea genome is close to being complete, although the number of genes may be inflated owing to the breaking of genes onto separate contings during the assembly process.</p><p>When compared to other sequenced plant genomes, such as those from cucumber (26,682)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Huang, S. et al. The genome of the cucumber, Cucumis sativus L. Nat. Genet. 41, 1275â1281 (2009)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1962">19</a></sup>, cacao (28,798)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1966">20</a></sup>, grapevine (29,585)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1970">14</a></sup> and <i>L. japonicus</i> (38,483)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1977">21</a></sup>, the number of predicted genes in the pigeonpea genome is higher, but comparable to poplar (45,555)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1982">22</a></sup>, soybean (46,430)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1986">12</a></sup> and <i>M. truncatula</i> (47,529) (Nevin Young, University of Minnesota, personal communication). Comparison of the features of pigeonpea genes with those of other dicot genomes indicates that they have similar characteristics, e.g., the size of mRNAs, coding sequences, exons and introns. However, the average number of exons per gene in the pigeonpea (3.59) is less than for soybean (5.80), whereas average exon (267.39 bp) and intron (536.89 bp) lengths are longer than those for soybean (216.13 bp exons and 419.43 bp introns) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>).</p><p>All predicted genes were functionally annotated following a consensus approach of either known homologous or predictive sequence signatures using Swissprot, GO, TrEMBL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6311e1999">23</a></sup>, InterPro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScanâan integration platform for the signaturerecognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847â848 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2003">24</a></sup> and KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27â30 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2007">25</a></sup>. The largest number of genes showed homology with proteins in TrEMBL (95.77%) followed by those in the InterPro (70.01%) database. In total, 46,750 (96.04%) genes had sufficient similarity to entries in databases to tentatively assign gene functions. Only 1,930 (3.96%) genes remain unannotated (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9</a>). In addition to protein-coding genes, we have identified 862 microRNA (miRNA), 763 tRNA, 329 rRNA and 363 small nuclear (snRNA) genes in the pigeonpea genome set (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). It is important to mention that rRNA genes in pigeonpea genome were predicted by aligning the 5.8S, 18S and 25S rRNA of <i>Arabidopsis</i> and 28S rRNA of rice against the pigeonpea genome assembly using BLASTN. We also determined the rDNA loci cytogenetically in the pigeonpea genome using fluorescence <i>in situ</i> hybridization (FISH) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Synteny with sequenced plant genomes</h3><p>The <i>Papilionoideae</i> subfamily, which contains pigeonpea and several other crop legumes, diverged into two major subgroups, the millettioid and galegoid clades, <span class="stix">â¼</span>54 million years (Myr) ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Lavin, M., Herendeen, P.S. &amp; Wojciechowski, M.F. Evolutionary rates analysis of Leguminosae implicates a rapid diversification of lineages during the tertiary. Syst. Biol. 54, 575â594 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2038">26</a></sup>. Within the millettioid clade, pigeonpea diverged from soybean <span class="stix">â¼</span>20â30 Myr ago. In spite of this long period of divergence, high levels of synteny are observed between the millettioid species pigeonpea and soybean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) as well as between pigeonpea and the galegoid species <i>M. truncatula</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>) and <i>L. japonicus</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Details of these syntenic blocks are provided in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11.</a> Each pigeonpea chromosome shows extensive synteny with two or more than two chromosomes in soybean, likely due to the independent duplication event in soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2064">12</a></sup> following divergence from pigeonpea. Even with this duplication event, the level of synteny and the blocks themselves are prominent (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Extensive synteny between the pigeonpea and soybean genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Extensive synteny between the pigeonpea and soybean genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig1_HTML.jpg" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="697"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Soybean pseudomolecules, labeled as Gm, are represented as green boxes. Numbers along each chromosome box are sequence length in megabases. Pigeonpea pseudomolecules, labeled as CcLG, are shown with each chromosome as a different color. Syntenic blocks were identified through reciprocal best matches between gene models and block identification using i-ADHoRe. Each line radiating from a pigeonpea pseudomolecule represents a gene match found in a block between soybean and pigeonpea.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Absence of recent genome duplication</h3><p>We analyzed gene content and gene order, using the i-ADHoRe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Simillion, C., Janssens, K., Sterck, L. &amp; van de Peer, Y. i-ADHoRe 2.0: an improved tool to detect degenerated genomic homology using genomic profiles. Bioinformatics 24, 127â128 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2100">27</a></sup> tool to find syntenic blocks by identifying successive pairs of duplicated genes. Using the same parameter set as used in the soybean genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2104">12</a></sup>, we identified a total of 28 duplicated syntenic blocks in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 10</a>). The number of homologs (gene pairs) within a block averaged 6, with a range from 4 to 18. Interestingly, chromosome 11 seems to be highly fragmented with block matches to five other chromosomes (CcLG02, CcLG03, CcLG06, CcLG08 and CcLG10). We also found three intrachromosomal duplications, two of which were located in CcLG06, with the third present in the chromosome CcLG11. Although chromosome number can be reduced after polyploidization, the chromosome number of pigeonpea (2<i>n</i> = 22) relative to other diploid legumes, also supports the lack of a genome duplication event in pigeonpea (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). This contrasts with soybean, the only other member of the <i>Phaseoleae</i> for which a genome sequence is available<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2121">12</a></sup>. Comparison of the pigeonpea and soybean genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Fig1">Fig. 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) confirms the recent whole-genome duplication <span class="stix">â¼</span>13 Myr ago in the soybean genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2131">12</a></sup>, which is missing in the pigeonpea genome.</p><p>Moreover, no major duplications (similar to the duplication of chromosomes 5â8) found in <i>M. truncatula</i> (Nevin Young, personal communication) were retained from the 58 Myr ago whole-genome duplication that occurred in all members of <i>Papilionoideae</i>. These observations indicate that there was most likely a period of extensive rearrangements after the whole-genome duplication 58 Myr ago, which stabilized before the split between the millettioid and galegoid clades 54 Myr ago, and that some of these rearrangements are lineage specific. However, as expected, local rearrangements have occurred during the course of evolution. For instance, this is seen in the microsynteny between chromosome CcLG06 of pigeonpea and chromosome 1 of soybean (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Fig2">Fig. 2</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Microsynteny analysis between pigeonpea and soybean genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Microsynteny analysis between pigeonpea and soybean genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="497"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>One chromosome arm of soybean chromosome 01S (south arm) and pigeonpea CcLG06 (indicated as a green circle in the whole-genome dot-plot in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a>) is shown here as a representation of microsynteny. Mapping of the pigeonpea transcriptome assembly contigs (TACs) of the pigeonpea transcriptome assembly (CcTA v2) onto both genomes (indicated by green lines) was used as a measure of conserved gene order. (<b>a</b>) The first part shows local rearrangements. (<b>b</b>) The later part indicates very good collinearity among genes in the two genomes.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Comparisons of gene families among eudicots</h3><p>We used several strategies to identify pigeonpea-specific gene families. For instance, we used protein sequence similarities to cluster gene families from all of the sequenced legume genomes (<i>M. truncatula</i>, soybean, <i>L. japonicus</i> and pigeonpea), using grapevine as an out-group (nonlegume) species. This revealed 4,311 clusters of genes, containing 72,193 genes that are common to all five eudicot genomes, 903 clusters containing 7,513 genes found only in the four legume genomes, 1,024 clusters specific to soybean and pigeonpea, and 3,068 gene families with 15,076 genes specific to the pigeonpea genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#Fig3">Fig. 3</a>). About 31% of pigeonpea genes are specific to pigeonpea. However, because a majority of these encode hypothetical proteins supported only at the transcript level (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 12</a>), this is probably an overestimate. In fact, InterPro annotation of the 15,076 genes revealed that 6,714 are of unknown function, whereas 4,788 are transposon related (reverse transcriptase, retrotransposon gag protein, transposon and transposase) and the remaining 3,574 have other functions. Analysis of the proteomes for the five eudicot species using Gene Ontology (GO) and InterPro terms revealed differences between pigeonpea and the other four species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 12</a>). Some of these differences may reflect the evolutionary history of pigeonpea or its adaptations to specific environments.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Distribution of gene families among five eudicot genomes (M. truncatula, soybean, L. japonicus, pigeonpea and grapevine)."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Distribution of gene families among five eudicot genomes (<i>M. truncatula</i>, soybean, <i>L. japonicus</i>, pigeonpea and grapevine).</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fnbt.2022/MediaObjects/41587_2012_Article_BFnbt2022_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="668"></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>Homologous genes in pigeonpea, soybean, <i>M. truncatula</i>, <i>L. japonicus</i> and grapevine were clustered to gene families. The numbers of gene families are indicated for each species and species intersection.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/nbt.2022/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">ORFan genes</h3><p>The previous section identifies a large number of genes (15,076) that could not be grouped into gene families with genes from the other three sequenced legume genomes (or the grapevine genome) and therefore occupy pigeonpea-specific gene families (some of which could be ORFan genes). ORFan genes are protein-encoding genes that have no significant sequence similarity to any other proteins and/or peptides in the genome or protein databases outside the taxon of interest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Brcic-Kostic, K. Neutral mutation as the source of genetic variation in life history traits. Genet. Res. 86, 53â63 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2241">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Wilson, G.A. et al. Orphans as taxonomically restricted and ecologically important genes. Microbiology 151, 2499â2501 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2244">29</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Schmid, K. &amp; Aquadro, C. The evolutionary analysis of âorphansâ from the Drosophila genome identifies rapidly diverging and incorrectly annotated genes. Genetics 159, 589â598 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2247">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Donoghue, M.T. et al. Evolutionary origins of Brassicaceae specific genes in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 47 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2250">31</a></sup>. ORFan genes may represent lineage-specific adaptations (or innovations), such as in stress responses, as highlighted as a key feature of ORFans in both rice<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Guo, W.J., Li, P., Ling, J. &amp; Ye, S.P. Significant comparative characteristics between orphan and nonorphan genes in the rice (Oryza sativa L.) genome. Comp. Funct. Genomics 2007, 21676 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2254">32</a></sup> and <i>Arabidopsis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Donoghue, M.T. et al. Evolutionary origins of Brassicaceae specific genes in Arabidopsis thaliana. BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 47 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2260">31</a></sup>. To identify pigeonpea ORFan genes, we used a BLAST filtering approach involving sequence data from all available taxa against the 48,680 pigeonpea genes to identify 266 gene models restricted to the tribe <i>Phaseoleae</i>. Of the 266 <i>Phaseoleae</i>-restricted ORFans, 97 ORFans had significant sequence similarity (e &lt; 0.0001) to peptides from soybean, leaving 169 putative pigeonpea-specific ORFan genes. The <i>Phaseoleae</i>-restricted ORFans in pigeonpea display many of the characteristics of ORFans identified in other species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Taylor, J.S. &amp; Raes, J. Duplication and divergence: the evolution of new genes and old ideas. Annu. Rev. Genet. 38, 615â643 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2274">33</a></sup>, namely short length, few introns and unusual GC content (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 13</a>).</p><p>To determine the evolutionary origin of the pigeonpea ORFans, we identified significant hits to conserved (non-ORFan) pigeonpea genes by performing all-against-all BLASTP searches (e &lt; 0.0001). Of the 266 ORFans, 202 have at least one significant hit to a non-ORFan gene with a mean percentage coverage of alignment of 84.38 Â± 24.3541. To identify sequence matches to non-ORFans in different reading frames, we performed all-against-all BLASTN searches (e &lt; 0.0001) on coding sequences. This identified an additional 11 ORFans with at least one significant sequence match, representing out-of-frame hits with mean percentage coverage of alignment of 87.6 Â± 22.6854. These data indicate that the vast majority of ORFans (213) in the pigeonpea genome are duplicates, which have evolved by a duplication-divergence model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Taylor, J.S. &amp; Raes, J. Duplication and divergence: the evolution of new genes and old ideas. Annu. Rev. Genet. 38, 615â643 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2284">33</a></sup>. Of the remaining 53 ORFans, 11 have evolved due to frameshifts generating novel open reading frames (as identified by genes with coding sequence hits to soybean) and two ORFans originated either <i>de novo</i> or as a result of gene loss (as identified by intergenic hits in soybean). The remaining 40 ORFans have no identifiable sequence similarity to any sequence tested, making it impossible to discern their evolutionary origins.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Large-scale identification of genetic markers</h3><p>Narrow genetic diversity, coupled with limited genomic resources, has been a major bottleneck for applying molecular plant breeding for improvement of pigeonpea. Simple sequence repeat (SSR) and singlenucleotide polymorphism (SNP) markers are currently the markers of choice for plant breeding. Analysis of the pigeonpea genome provided a total of 309,052 SSRs (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 14</a>). For designing SSR primers, 29,467 sequences containing tri-, tetra-, penta-, hexa- or compound repeat units, which are generally polymorphic, were considered and 23,410 primer pairs were successfully designed that can be converted into genetic markers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 15</a>).</p><p>The genome assembly was also used to align 128.9 million Illumina transcript reads from 12 different pigeonpea genotypes that are parents of 6 mapping populations for identification of SNPs<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dubey, A. et al. Defining the transcriptome assembly and its use for genome dynamics and transcriptome profiling studies in pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan L.). DNA Res. 18, 153â164 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2308">34</a></sup>. By aligning the transcript reads for a given parental combination onto the pigeonpea genome assembly, we identified sequence variants that differ between the parental combinations. The number of SNPs between two parental genotypes ranged from 2,164 (BSMR 736 Ã TAT 10) to 16,651 (ICP 28 Ã ICPL 87091) (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 16</a>). In total, we identified 28,104 novel SNPs across the 12 genotypes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM3">Supplementary Table 17</a>). These SNPs can be used for germplasm characterization and for genetic improvement. It is important to mention that SNP calling between the high-quality sequence reads and the draft genome assembly showed a heterozygosity rate of only 0.067% in the Asha genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 18</a>), confirming the inbred nature of the Asha accession that was used for genome sequencing.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec11" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec11-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec11">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec11-content"><p>Recently, draft genome sequences have become available for two model legume species, <i>M. truncatula</i> (Nevin Young, personal communication) and <i>L. japonicus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Sato, S. et al. Genome structure of the legume, Lotus japonicus. DNA Res. 15, 227â239 (2008)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2335">21</a></sup>, and one industrial legume crop, soybean<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2339">12</a></sup>. This report presents the genome of the first orphan legume crop and the second food legume (after soybean). Pigeonpea plays a substantial role in the livelihood of resource-poor smallholder farmers in marginal environments. Fungal diseases (e.g., FW), viral diseases (e.g., SMD) and insect pests (e.g., <i>Helicoverpa armigera</i> (pod borer)), together with abiotic stresses such as salinity and water logging, have limited the yield of pigeonpea to about one-third of the potential yield. Moreover, pigeonpea crop productivity has remained stagnant for the last 50 years. Low genetic diversity, coupled with availability of only a few hundred useful markers, has hampered the development of intraspecific genetic maps for identification of markers associated with quantitative trait loci for resistance and/or tolerance to these yield drags. As a result, pigeonpea breeders have not been able to increase the varietal yields. However, pigeonpea is the first legume to have hybrid varieties released based on cytoplasmic-nuclear male sterility<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Stokstad, E. The plant breeder and the pea. Science 316, 196â197 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2346">35</a></sup>. Improvement of parental lines for biotic and abiotic resistance stresses as well as maintaining the purity of hybrid seeds is critical for sustainable hybrid production.</p><p>The availability of a draft genome sequence opens new avenues for pigeonpea improvement. In the short-term, the genome sequence will usher the pigeonpea crop into the molecular breeding era by deploying the SSR and SNP markers identified for genetic mapping and trait identification. Breeding approaches such as marker-assisted recurrent selection and genomic selection will now be feasible for pigeonpea breeding, and may be even further advanced by genotyping by sequencing that can be done with the help of the draft genome sequence. In the long term, with the help of low-cost sequencing technologies or approaches, the draft genome sequence will facilitate understanding of the genetic basis of many traits at genome level and allow the undertaking of genome-wide association studies involving thousands of pigeonpea accessions (13,632 accessions in ICRISAT genebank)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Bohra, A. et al. Harnessing the potential of crop wild relatives through genomics tools for pigeonpea improvement. J. Plant Biol. 37, 85â100 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2353">7</a></sup>. It will lead to the identification and manipulation of candidate genes or genomic regions to enable breeding of varieties or hybrids resistant or tolerant to biotic and abiotic stresses as well as global climate fluctuations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Varshney, R. et al. Agricultural biotechnology for crop improvement in a variable climate: hope or hype? Trends Plant Sci. 16, 363â371 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2357">36</a></sup>.</p><p>For example, one of the most attractive features of pigeonpea relative to other legume crops is its tolerance of drought stress. As a preliminary screen to begin to understand the genetic basis of this drought tolerance, we analyzed 511 universal drought-responsive protein sequences from the <i>Viridiplantae</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Isokpehi, D.R. et al. Identification of drought-responsive universal stress proteins in Viridiplantae. Bioinform Biol. Insights 5, 41â58 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2366">37</a></sup> in the sequenced legume genomes. Of the 511 proteins, 427 had homologous sequences in the legumes. Pigeonpea had a higher number (111) than either <i>M. truncatula</i> (90; Nevin Young, personal communication) or <i>L. japonicus</i> (58) (data not shown). It had a similar number of drought-responsive genes to the number found in soybean (109), but soybean underwent a recent genome duplication event. These genes need to be confirmed experimentally but are, nonetheless, candidates that can be used to begin to gain insight into the genetic architecture of pigeonpea's drought tolerance and for screens to identify superior haplotypes for improvement (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 19</a>).</p><p>In addition to bioinformatics-based comparative and predictive approaches at the genomic level, differential gene expression studies through RNA-Seq will facilitate the identification of candidate genes for biotic and abiotic stresses, for example, resistance to <i>Helicoverpa armigera</i>, SMD and FW. These candidate genes will be critical for the improvement of pigeonpea and perhaps other crops. In brief, the availability of a pigeonpea reference genome sequence will facilitate greater integration of biotechnological tools into pigeonpea breeding efforts to minimize the yield gap in farmer's fields in Asia and Africa. The availability of pigeonpea genome sequence will also facilitate assembly and alignment of genomes of other <i>Phaseoloid</i> species, such as cowpea and common bean. These comparative approaches may allow other legumes to leverage the unique characteristics found in pigeonpea. For example, candidate genes associated with drought tolerance and the cytoplasmic-nuclear male-sterility system that are unique to pigeonpea may be used for improving other legume crops such as soybean and common bean that are adversely affected by drought stress and possibly bring hybrid seed production in other legume species.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">High-molecular-weight DNA preparation.</h3><p>High-quality genomic DNA was prepared from seeds of the Asha variety. Seeds were grown in a dark chamber for 2 weeks before harvest. A standard phenol/chloroform method of DNA extraction was performed. The extracted DNA was treated with RNase A and proteinase K, respectively, to prevent RNA and protein contamination, and further precipitated with ethanol.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Whole-genome shotgun sequencing.</h3><p>We used a whole-genome shotgun sequencing strategy with Illumina Genome Analyzer sequencing technology and Hiseq 2000 Sequencing System. To get enough DNA for the library construction and sequencing, we carried out whole-genome amplification. We constructed a total of 22 paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes of about 180 base pairs (bp), 250 bp, 350 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb. In total, we generated 237.2 Gb data of paired-ends ranging from 50 to 100 bp. To reduce the effect of sequencing error to the assembly, we have taken a series of checking and filtering steps on reads generated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311â317 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2408">10</a></sup>. Using stringent criteria, only 130.7 Gb data were considered for <i>de novo</i> genome assembly.</p><p>The genome size was calculated using the total length of sequence reads divided by sequencing depth. To estimate the sequencing depth, we counted the copy number of a certain K-mer (e.g., 17-mer) present in sequence reads, and plotted the distribution of copy numbers. The peak value of the frequency curve represents the overall sequencing depth. We used the algorithm: (<i>N</i> Ã (<i>L</i> â <i>K</i> + 1) â <i>B)/D</i> = <i>G</i>, where <i>N</i> is the total sequence read number, <i>L</i> is the average length of sequence reads and <i>K</i> is K-mer length, defined as 17 bp here. To minimize the influence of sequencing error, K-mers with low frequency (&lt;4) are discarded. <i>B</i> is the total number of low frequency 17-mer. <i>G</i> denotes the genome size, and <i>D</i> is the overall depth estimated from K-mer distribution.</p><p>We carried out the whole-genome assembly using SOAPdenovo for the remaining reads after the above filtering and correction steps<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2455">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311â317 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2458">10</a></sup>. The contigs after SOAPdenovo corrections were formed without any gap. We realigned all the usable reads onto the contig sequences and obtained aligned paired ends. We then calculated the amount of shared paired-end relationships between each pair of contigs, weighted the rate of consistent and conflicting paired ends, and then constructed the scaffolds step by step, from short insert-sized paired ends, to long insert-sized paired ends. Subsequently, 88,860 BAC end sequences were generated from two BAC libraries of Asha. All the raw paired BAC end sequences (88,860) were filtered against RepeatMasker. Subsequently, 41,302 paired-BAC end sequences that passed after filtering were used for mapping to scaffolds to obtain the super scaffolds. Finally, we used the genetic map (<i>C. cajan</i> ICP 28 Ã <i>C. scaraboides</i> ICPW 94) comprising 833 marker loci including 209 BAC end sequenceâderived SSR markers and 624 conserved orthologous sequenceâbased markers based on legume transcript sequence for developing the final scaffolds or pseudomolecules. To close the gaps inside the constructed scaffolds, which were mainly composed of repeats that were masked before scaffold construction, we used the paired-end information to retrieve the read pairs that had one end mapped to the unique contig and the other located in the gap region, then did a local assembly for these collected reads.</p><p>The quality of genome assembly for microbial contamination was checked by its analysis against a database of bacterial genomes using Megablast (E-value &lt; 1e-5, &gt; 90% identity, &gt; 200 bp length mapped to scaffold sequence). For checking the contamination of assembly with organellar DNA, soybean chloroplast DNA (152,218 bp) downloaded from <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/DQ317523">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/DQ317523</a> was screened against the pigeonpea genome assembly.</p><p>For checking the completeness of the assembly, a transcriptome assembly comprising 21,434 unigenes, referred to as transcriptome assembly contigs (TACs) and defined based on 10,817 Sanger ESTs, 2.19 million 454/FLX transcript reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dubey, A. et al. Defining the transcriptome assembly and its use for genome dynamics and transcriptome profiling studies in pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan L.). DNA Res. 18, 153â164 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2482">34</a></sup> and 128.91 million Illumina transcript reads<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Dubey, A. et al. Defining the transcriptome assembly and its use for genome dynamics and transcriptome profiling studies in pigeonpea (Cajanus cajan L.). DNA Res. 18, 153â164 (2011)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2486">34</a></sup> was used for mapping the TACs to the assembly genome with the help of BLAT software. Analysis was done at different criteria of percent sequence homology and percent coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/nbt.2022#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Identification of repetitive elements.</h3><p>There are two main types of repeats in the genome (tandem repeats and interspersed repeats). We searched the genome for tandem repeats using Tandem Repeats Finder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573â580 (1999)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2501">38</a></sup> and Repbase (composed of many transposable elements) to identify the interspersed repeats. Transposable elements in the genome assembly were identified both at the DNA and protein level. For identification of transposable elements at the DNA level, RepeatMasker was applied using a custom library comprising a combination of Repbase and the <i>de novo</i> transposable element library of the pigeonpea genome. At the protein level, RepeatProteinMask, updated software in the RepeatMasker package, was used to perform RM-BlastX against the transposable elements protein database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462â467 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2508">39</a></sup>. In this context, we used the software RepeatModeler to build a new repeat library based on the genome. These results were used to construct a new library for RepeatMasker and RepeatMasker was run again to find homolog repeats in the genome. Identified repeats were classified into different known classes as per standard genome analysis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2512">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Price, A.L., Jones, N.C. &amp; Pevzner, P.A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351âi358 (2005)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2515">40</a></sup>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Gene prediction and annotation.</h3><p>To predict genes, we used three main approaches: homology-based method (H), <i>de novo</i> method (D) and EST/unigenes-based method (C). Results of these three methods were integrated by the GLEAN program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2530">17</a></sup> and then filtered multiple times and checked manually.</p><p>Protein sequences from six sequenced eudicot species, namely <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Cucumis sativus</i>, <i>Carica papaya</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i> and <i>Glycine max</i>, were used to perform prediction, taking one species each time. We mapped them to the genome assembly using TblastN with E-value - 1e-5 (ref. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kent, W.J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2555">41</a>). After this, homologous genome sequences were aligned against the matching proteins using GeneWise (version 2.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Birney, E., Clamp, M. &amp; Durbin, R. GeneWise and Genomewise. Genome Res. 14, 988â995 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2559">42</a></sup> for accurate spliced alignments. Subsequently, we filtered pseudogenes from the homology-search results from six data sets.</p><p>For <i>de novo</i> prediction, Augustus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Stanke, M. et al. Augustus: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, 435â439 (2006)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2569">43</a></sup>, GENSCAN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Salamov, A.A. &amp; Solovyev, V.V. Ab initio gene finding in Drosophila genomic DNA. Genome Res. 10, 516â522 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2573">44</a></sup> and GlimmerHMM<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Majoros, W.H., Pertea, M. &amp; Salzberg, S.L. TigrScan and GlimmerHMM: two opensource ab initio eukaryotic gene-finders. Bioinformatics 20, 2878â2879 (2004)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2577">45</a></sup> were used to predict genes with parameters trained on <i>A. thaliana</i>. We merged three <i>de novo</i> predictions into a unigene set. <i>De novo</i> gene models that were supported by two or more <i>de novo</i> methods were retained. For overlapping gene models, the longest one was selected and finally, we got <i>de novo</i>-based gene models (43,647).</p><p>In the third approach, we used the transcribed sequences, that is, 10,376 Sanger ESTs to align against the genome assembly using BLAT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kent, W.J. BLATâthe BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656â664 (2002)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2600">41</a></sup> to generate spliced alignments, and then filtered the overlaps to link the spliced alignments using PASA (<a href="http://www.lerner.ccf.org/moleccard/qin/pasa/">http://www.lerner.ccf.org/moleccard/qin/pasa/</a>). As a result, 2,246 genes were defined.</p><p>Finally, using one <i>de novo</i> set (43,647) and six homolog-based results as gene models (33,360 to 39,749), together with an EST-based gene set (2,246), integration was done using the GLEAN program<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Elsik, C.G. et al. Creating a honey bee consensus gene set. Genome Biol. 8, R13 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2618">17</a></sup>. Finally, we got the GLEAN gene set (referred to as G-set, 48,369).</p><p>Sixty-nine genes, with high GeneWise scores, complete ORFs, from the homology-based prediction, which were not included in the G-set but were supported by ESTs/unigenes, were added to the GLEAN result (48,438 genes, termed the GH-set). Subsequently, three <i>de novo</i> gene sets based on homology-search analysis were compared with the GH-set. In cases where there was an overlap between two or more genes in these gene sets, the longest gene was selected. Subsequently, the gene set was translated using SwissProt and EST/unigene results translated into proteins and only those genes that got support as mentioned above and did not have any overlap with GH-set were selected. At this point also, we filtered 14 genes that have a coding sequence length of &lt;150 bp or N content &gt;50%, the genes that have internal stop codon or frameshift. And subsequently, we got a gene set, referred to as GHD-set, comprising 48,671 genes.</p><p>For checking the completeness of the gene set defined, the core eukaryotic gene-mapping approach (CEGMA)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061â1067 (2007)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2631">18</a></sup> that rapidly assess genome completeness and gene structure prediction was used with the gene set defined. CEGMA analysis includes a set of 453 core genes that are supposed to be highly conserved and single-copy genes present in all eukaryotes. Based on this analysis, a set of 9 genes that did not align with any gene defined was also included in the final gene set of 48,680 genes, referred to as Official Gene Set (OGSv1.0).</p><p>Gene functions were assigned according to the best match of the alignments using BLASTP (1e-5) to SwissProt<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The SWISS-PROT protein sequence database and its supplement TrEMBL in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45â48 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2638">23</a></sup> and TrEMBL databases. InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Zdobnov, E.M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScanâan integration platform for the signaturerecognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847â848 (2001)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2642">24</a></sup> determined motifs and domains of genes against protein databases including Pfam, PRINTS, PROSITE, ProDom and SMART<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Ashburner, M. et al. Gene Ontology: tool for the unification of biology. Nat. Genet. 25, 25â29 (2000)." href="/articles/nbt.2022#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d6311e2646">46</a></sup>. Gene Ontology IDs for each gene were obtained from the corresponding InterPro entry. All genes were aligned against KEGG proteins<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes.
